  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8c03e372-0cc0-4ea1-b9e1-89d04f4d1b8e'
      });

<div id="review_3490158010" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3490158010" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3490158010?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782274513" href="/book/show/37678644-amok">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=swedish-narration">swedish-narration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible-uk">audible-uk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3490158010" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10452513275344821823">This grabs you and doesn’t let you go!<br/>It’s short and powerful.<br/>View it as a psychological thriller.<br/><br/>In 1912 on an ocean-liner traveling from India to Europe, the novella’s unnamed narrator goes up on deck in the middle of the night to escape his sweltering, tiny, claustrophobic cabin. There, at first, he perceives only the beautifully lit night sky illuminated by the moon and brilliant stars. This contrasts with the rolling, dark waves below. Later, nearby him on the deck, he discerns a dark silho</span>
  <span id="freeText10452513275344821823" style="display:none">This grabs you and doesn’t let you go!<br />It’s short and powerful.<br />View it as a psychological thriller.<br /><br />In 1912 on an ocean-liner traveling from India to Europe, the novella’s unnamed narrator goes up on deck in the middle of the night to escape his sweltering, tiny, claustrophobic cabin. There, at first, he perceives only the beautifully lit night sky illuminated by the moon and brilliant stars. This contrasts with the rolling, dark waves below. Later, nearby him on the deck, he discerns a dark silhouette of a figure, two reflected circles from a pair of glasses and the glowing ember from a pipe. An all-enveloping atmosphere engulfs the reader from the start. <br /><br />The prose makes the book remarkable, way, way above the ordinary. The atmosphere drawn is beautiful, captivating, enticing—it makes the reader <b>need</b> to know more. One begs for the whole story. The man there with the pipe opens his heart and tells his story to the novella’s unnamed narrator. Concerning the plot, I need say nothing more….except one thing. The figure with the pipe is a doctor. <br /><br />Passion, different sorts of passion, lie at the center of the story. Passion can be felt between two people. It can also be felt for one’s occupation and for an ideal.<br /><br />Now, look at the title. “Amok”, sometimes expressed as “running amok”, is a concept taken from Southeast Asian Austronesian languages / cultures. It means to behave in a frenzied, out-of-control or unrestrained manner. Some classify such behavior as a psychological disorder. What is important to know here is that the man our narrator encounters there in the night on the ship’s deck has been and is caught up in just such a frenzy. What has put the man in such a frenzy and what will he do? <br /><br />I listened to a Swedish translation of the novella. Hugo Hultenberg did the translation form German to Swedish. What I can state is that the prose was absolutely wonderful—stunning, emotive, captivating, enthralling. I wonder and hope the German to English translation is as good. <br /><br />The Swedish language audiobook I listened to is read by Martin Halland. His narration I have given five stars. He speaks slowly and clearly. You hear very word and cannot help but be drawn in. If you know Swedish, I highly recommend the Swedish audiobook. In Swedish, the book’s title is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/43528760.Amokl_paren" title="Amoklöparen by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Amoklöparen</a>.<br /><br />At GR, the novella is also registered here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18073944-amok">https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1...</a><br /><br />This is fantastic. Read it!<br /><br />********************<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37678644.Amok" title="Amok by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Amok</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4455573.Burning_Secret" title="Burning Secret by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Burning Secret</a> 4 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59149.Beware_of_Pity" title="Beware of Pity by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Beware of Pity</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday" title="The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">The World of Yesterday</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24456813.Letter_From_An_Unknown_Woman" title="Letter From An Unknown Woman by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Letter From An Unknown Woman</a> 3 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28364718.Summer_Before_the_Dark_Stefan_Zweig_and_Joseph_Roth__Ostend_1936" title="Summer Before the Dark Stefan Zweig and Joseph Roth, Ostend 1936 by Volker Weidermann" rel="nofollow noopener">Summer Before the Dark: Stefan Zweig and Joseph Roth, Ostend 1936</a> 3 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="10452513275344821823" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3620092383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13335794645736676606">This was an intense 88 page read and, as with the other Zweigs I’ve read, is a compelling well-written story. However, this one evoked largely uncomfortable feelings that made this story not quite as satisfying as most other Zweigs. This is largely due to the thoughts and actions of the doctor who is relating his story to our narrator in the dark of night on an ocean liner sailing back to Europe from India, southeast Asia and Australia. <br/>At first I found the doctor character fairly unusual. On re</span>
  <span id="freeText13335794645736676606" style="display:none">This was an intense 88 page read and, as with the other Zweigs I’ve read, is a compelling well-written story. However, this one evoked largely uncomfortable feelings that made this story not quite as satisfying as most other Zweigs. This is largely due to the thoughts and actions of the doctor who is relating his story to our narrator in the dark of night on an ocean liner sailing back to Europe from India, southeast Asia and Australia. <br />At first I found the doctor character fairly unusual. On reflection, I thought he could have come from some Joseph Conrad novella as Conrad often populates his works with Europeans with strange motivations hiding away in southeast Asia. The doctor tells his tale of ‘running amok’ but his attitudes and desires are unsympathetic and may cause some readers to dislike the book. I found the book disturbing yet timely in view of the current state of the U.S. Supreme Court. <br />Thought provoking and intense but not quite as rewarding as other Zweigs I’ve read; still a 4 star read that is well-worth two hours of your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13335794645736676606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12500494191905887659">I find myself so conflicted with so many of these #Reading1001 books. <tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review3117188753">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2836834215" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2836834215" />
    <a title="Jason" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10615847-jason"><img alt="Jason" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566750485p2/10615847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2836834215?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason" href="/user/show/10615847-jason">Jason</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782274513" href="/book/show/37678644-amok">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2836834215" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9978517740478218141">Stefan Zweig, an Austrian Jew born into an extremely wealthy and cultured Viennese family, was an immensely popular writer of short fiction and biographies in the 1920s and 1930s, read throughout the Occidental world. A friend of Sigmund Freud and Arthur Schnitzler, he in many ways represented an Old European sensibility on the cusp, his fiction in large part linked to a tradition leading back to Guy de Maupassant, 19th century master of short fiction engaged with grim fate and social determinis</span>
  <span id="freeText9978517740478218141" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig, an Austrian Jew born into an extremely wealthy and cultured Viennese family, was an immensely popular writer of short fiction and biographies in the 1920s and 1930s, read throughout the Occidental world. A friend of Sigmund Freud and Arthur Schnitzler, he in many ways represented an Old European sensibility on the cusp, his fiction in large part linked to a tradition leading back to Guy de Maupassant, 19th century master of short fiction engaged with grim fate and social determinism. Having fled Europe during the rise of the Nazis, Zweig and his second wife would die in an apparent double suicide in 1942, roughly halfway through the Second World War, immediately after Stefan completed his memoirs, confounded by the trajectory of history, grieving a world they had mistakenly thought they knew, that they perhaps once did. Stefan Zweig was an aristocrat from an apparently disappeared world, an involuntary exile wracked by devastating disillusionment. If we look back to his fiction from the 20s we immediately become aware of a writer who has long had a sensitivity to the intimate workings of anguish, his characters having a general tendency to give themselves over misguidedly to the most unavailing romantic devotions, to submit to the tortures of their ardour, and be broken at the wheel of love. Before I had ever read Zweig, I had seen the 1948 Max Ophüls adaptation of his novella LETTER FROM AN UNKNOWN WOMAN, had in fact seen it numerous times, as it had become one of my two or three favourite films of the Classical Hollywood era. It is a beautiful and devastating film of extraordinary artistry. It is also, as it would turn out, a somewhat sanitized adaptation of the 1922 novella, a novella which I did eventually get to and which is notably frank about its heroine’s temporarily having resorted to sex work in addition to some of the aberrant extremes of her conduct more generally. When speaking of Freud, Schnitzler, and Zweig as Old World sensibilities at the cusp, what we mean in terms of the ‘cusp’ very much has to do with their frankness as regards sexual matters and the setting of new precedents as such. I have had a tendency to see in Schnitzler’s fiction evidence of a fatuous patriarch (you will note my extremely negative Goodreads review of FRÄULEIN ELSE), and if Zweig covers similar ground, he has tended to strike me as the more empathic analyst, far more flexible in his ability to genuinely identify with characters dissimilar to himself, especially with women. (I have also read Zweig's biography of the explorer Magellan, and am confident that Schnitzler would have been completely incapable of a work of such erudition, thoughtfulness, and committed scholarship.) Zweig's novella AMOK, originally published in 1922, the same year as LETTER FROM AN UNKNOWN WOMAN, presents an extremely interesting case, as it very much assesses the subject-position of a kind of fatuous patriarch (though a man who considered as such has to be deemed an abject failure), but does so in what I perceive to be subversive and enlightening terms, nonetheless demonstrating itself capable of deploying its critical methodology whilst simultaneously generating remarkable pathos. The novella is narrated by an unnamed gentleman who begins by noting that he possesses special insight regarding a news item from some years past, the specifics regarding which go unmentioned until the final pages, pertaining to an event that occurred in Naples harbour in March of 1912 (a decade before the publication of the story). The narrator goes on to detail a pair of encounters with a tormented doctor he met aboard a ship named OCEANIA upon which both men were returning to Europe from India. The narrator is something of a detached observer by nature, a man of a certain amount of circumspection, an alienated intelligence, not entirely comfortable with the company of his fellows. He meets the doctor late at night, away from the frivolity and distasteful merriment of the crowd. The doctor is clearly experiencing some species of torment, drinking heavily and dropping ominous hints. Over the course of two nights he will tell the narrator his tragic story. Naturally it involves a woman. The doctor is himself a man with a yen for isolating himself. He has been working for the Dutch practicing medicine at some remote backwater outpost. A woman entered the picture. A white woman, an English woman. The woman, veiled, attempts to rather forcefully compel him to provide her a service. I am reluctant to give too much away regarding the story, getting into the spoiler business et cetera, but I feel it important to reveal that the service she desires him to provide is an abortion. She has chosen him because of his reputation for competence and the likelihood that he will be able to keep the procedure a secret. Again, the woman is forceful, brusque. Again, she keeps herself veiled. The doctor demands that she remove the veil. “I saw the kind of face I had feared to see, an impenetrable face, hard, controlled, a face of ageless beauty, a face with grey English eyes in which all seemed at peace, and yet behind which one could dream that all was passion.” The way the doctor frames things, he would have the narrator believe that a switch was flipped in his brain, his nervous system. The woman coolly offers the doctor money. Money in exchange for the service and his subsequent departure from the country. The doctor has a more sordid transaction in mind. The woman will have none of it. She leaves. But that switch has been flipped, so the doctor would have his interlocutor believe. Obsession. Mania, torment, and obsession. Asked by the doctor if he knows what the expression “running amok” means, the narrator says he believes that it has something to do with “a kind of intoxication affecting the Malays…” The doctor: “It’s more than intoxication…it’s madness, a sort of human rabies, an attack of murderous, pointless monomania that bears no comparison with ordinary alcohol poisoning.” The doctor has studied several cases and attempted to look at the phenomenon objectively. A man is one minute “drinking his brew, impassive, indifferent, apathetic,” when all of a sudden he “leaps to his feet, snatches his dagger and runs out into the street, going straight ahead of him, always straight ahead, with no idea of any destination. With his kris he strikes down anything that crosses his path, man or beast, and this murderous frenzy makes him even more deranged. He froths at the mouth as he runs, he howls like a lunatic…but he still runs and runs and runs, he doesn’t look right, he doesn’t look left, he just runs on screaming shrilly, brandishing his bloodstained kris as he forges straight ahead in that dreadful way. The people of the village know that no power can halt a man running amok, so they shout warnings ahead when they see him coming—‘Amok! Amok!’—and everyone flees…but he runs on without hearing, without seeing, striking down anything he meets…until he is either shot dead like a mad dog or collapses of his own accord, still frothing at the mouth…” This is what the doctor would have us believe happened. To him. Except, you know, sans froth. He pursued the woman in a state of depersonalized delirium. A number of tragedies came to pass. The final tragedy is that which was hinted at at the story’s beginning, has not yet occurred whilst the stories are being told, and is elaborated upon in the final pages. The doctor repeatedly reveals himself as an unsavoury individual. He is casually racist, dissipated, bilious, selfish, and conveniently selective in his consideration of consequences. He has framed his story in order to enflame his own self-pity and to obfuscate his own culpability. Presenting himself as a man who “ran amok,” he makes the case that his actions were outside of his control, but who is he trying to persuade? It is clear that he is in large part trying to persuade himself and failing to. Evasion of personal responsibility only brings him back to the imposition of its mandates. All this talk is a fatuous forestalling of action, dallying in the grip of fate, but a fate for which he himself has to answer, over which he himself must take ownership. He makes it clear that he does not want the narrator’s assistance and that the narrator’s sympathy can do nothing for him, stating portentously that “the one human right one has left is to die as one wishes, and keep well away from any stranger’s help.” I will say no more than that, provide no more spoilers. It is interesting to me that I read AMOK having just revisited Roman Polanski’s 1992 film BITTER MOON, a film about a man subjecting another man to stories aboard a cruise ship, a film I thought of as concerning the storytelling con as the last refuge of kink and based upon a 1981 Pascal Bruckner novel that may well have been in part inspired by Zweig’s novella. Both BITTER MOON and AFLOAT ask us to consider the malignant games we sometimes play when we seek to tell our stories, to tell them a certain way, in a certain context. This is the subversive core of AFLOAT. Yes, the doctor and his story are tragic, but they are also manipulative and cruel, evidence of a compromised spirit intent on taking hostages. Sometimes the plague passes itself on through stories. This is not storytelling that unburdens. No, it poisons. There is no absolution. There can be no absolution. What am I telling you? I am telling you that I am a piece of shit and the world is shit and I want you to be ill. I can’t go on and perhaps I can bring you to the realization that you shouldn’t either, nobody should. The reason AMOK is genuinely tragic, however, is because the doctor’s sickness is moral sickness and requires a moral resolution. So it goes. And of course let’s just end this by considering that if we want to talk about Zweig as an Old World sensibility at the cusp, we ought to note that this novella’s tragedy in large part stems from a sensitive and adult consideration of matters concerning women’s reproductive rights; I am writing this at a time when we continue reckoning with these things, bearing witness in our news cycles to venal forces intent on reintroducing prohibitions that would only increase in number and frequency these sorts of tragedies. It is generally men of dubious status who want to decree what is right and wrong in these matters, write it in stone. They will happily regale you endlessly with all manner of maddening bullshit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9978517740478218141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9919601412336376238">Where do I start?<br/><br/>This book is problematic in so many ways! I don't really understand, why the rating of the book is so high? I mean 4/5? Really?<br/><br/>So, this book is misogynic and racist and I really wasn't expecting this, since I read "Chess Story" by Stefan Zweig and I actually enjoyed it. He also calls the locals "animals", just because they aren't whit</span>
  <span id="freeText9919601412336376238" style="display:none">Where do I start?<br /><br />This book is problematic in so many ways! I don't really understand, why the rating of the book is so high? I mean 4/5? Really?<br /><br />So, this book is misogynic and racist and I really wasn't expecting this, since I read "Chess Story" by Stefan Zweig and I actually enjoyed it. However, the doctor is telling this story about what happened in India and he just keeps complaining, because he couldn't find any white people??? He also calls the locals "animals", just because they aren't white? Like wtf dude<br /><br />And as I already mentioned, the misogyny in this book is real as well - so, there is this woman, who needs his help. The woman seems very confident and obviously does not like to ask for help, so she just offers him money in exchange for his help, which he does not want to accept - instead, he wants her to prostitute herself in order for him to help her. She rejects him and guess what - he just keeps following her and won't leave her alone.<br /><br />Personally, I think that this book is quite problematic.</span>

<div id="review_2675967168" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2675967168" />
    <a title="Belinda Carvalho" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5958405-belinda-carvalho"><img alt="Belinda Carvalho" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514916503p2/5958405.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2675967168?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Belinda Carvalho" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Belinda Carvalho" href="/user/show/5958405-belinda-carvalho">Belinda Carvalho</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782274513" href="/book/show/37678644-amok">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5958405-belinda-carvalho?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5958405-belinda-carvalho?shelf=zweig">zweig</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2675967168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17588800934056625409">Totally melodramatic, totally filled with passion..totally Zweig! This is a great bite-sized novella, set on a liner destined from the colonies to Britain, a tortured passenger reveals the tale that is haunting him. As always with Zweig, it's a tale of a world long ago, with settings and issues that might not longer have such relevance to life today but that's not what I read Zweig for. I read him for the emotional intensity and the revelations of a world gone by. A perfect little book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3177603854" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3177603854" />
    <a title="Book Wormy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32493366-book-wormy"><img alt="Book Wormy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403592448p2/32493366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3177603854?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Book Wormy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Book Wormy" href="/user/show/32493366-book-wormy">Book Wormy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35163158-amok">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32493366-book-wormy?shelf=1001-read">1001-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32493366-book-wormy?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3177603854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2282720974462355173">Personally this wasn't a book for me the writing is melodramatic and the central character behaved in an completely unbelievable way. Add to that the casual mentions of "yellow people" of seeing a "fellow white" of "masters" and the abuse of a servant boy (perhaps slave is the better term) and I lost engagement. <br/><br/>Yes the race issues are a product of the time but the writer is asking the reader to feel sympathy for a doctor who confesses about a woman patient that:<br/><br/><i>"just a wish to break her pride, </i></span>
  <span id="freeText2282720974462355173" style="display:none">Personally this wasn't a book for me the writing is melodramatic and the central character behaved in an completely unbelievable way. Add to that the casual mentions of "yellow people" of seeing a "fellow white" of "masters" and the abuse of a servant boy (perhaps slave is the better term) and I lost engagement. <br /><br />Yes the race issues are a product of the time but the writer is asking the reader to feel sympathy for a doctor who confesses about a woman patient that:<br /><br /><i>"just a wish to break her pride, dominate her as a man"</i><br /><br />This is a woman who is in desperate need of help and this is how he thinks. Really?? We are then asked to believe that because she won't give in and degrade herself before him he becomes obsessed with saving her to the point where he views his life as hell:<br /><br /><i>"I am not a believing Christian any more...I don't believe in heaven or hell, and if hell does exist I am not afraid of it, for it can't be worse than those hours I passed between morning and evening"</i><br /><br />Personally I think good, if you hadn't been such a dick in the first place you wouldn't have ended up in "hell"<br /><br />The reason for this change of heart from dick to knight in shining armour is explained by <i>"a kind of intoxication affecting the Malays"</i><br /><br /><i>The people of the village know that no power can halt a man running Amok, so they shout warnings ahead when they see him coming."</i><br /><br />Well why didn't you say that in the first place because that makes everything alright. Really??<br /><br />Character issues aside the way the story is set up works well, secret meetings on a boat late at night, a desperate need to confess to someone, rejection of help and a final melodramatic action that takes place "off-screen" as it were did make for an interesting if over the top read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2282720974462355173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10250190850219723101" style="display:none">An incredible desperation history<br />Actually this time I want to begin to write the story of doctor who was focused on by the readers and the writer. As all of us know there has been a ship crash in 1912 and this catastrophe was full of secrets between three people. They were doctor, a patient woman and the husband of the patient woman.<br /> This doctor was exiled from Germany and he applied to work in India as a doctor to the Netherlands. Bit one day an unexpected guest visits him. It was a woman who comes from England. This woman offers him a new life if he carries on his job illegal but our doctor says that it is impossible in the point of view losing his compensation salary from the Netherlands. She insists on to do it but, whatever happens he doesn't accept. She understands him, and she leaves there arrogantly,nonetheless, the doctor understands that there is something wrong and tries to follow her until train station. Yet, his following way is different from normal people. He runs as fast as through the train station and destroys whatever or hits whoever he sees. Suddenly a child stopped him to block and he beat the child, and unfortunately he lost her indication. <br /> He immediately goes to see with a governor in his region to make an appointment in an another area but the governor says that it is impossible right away and he has to wait. But if that night he comes to ball he can think a way making appointment nevertheless he thinks that she comes to ball and Hope's to see her in there. At 7 o'clock he's been in ball before everything is not ready. But he wanted to be there on time and as he wanted he was there. When he saw her for the first time he was affected,because she was extremely beautiful and she danced with a young soldier on the dance floor and she left the hall. While she was leaving, the doctor followed him and called her and when she looked him back, she smiled and thanked to him because of the unwritten receipt by him.<br /> A few hours later her servant boy visited the doctor and said that she got worsen and the doctor moved on and saw her bloody body of her. He tried everything to save her life but everything was in vain. Later her boyfriend who is a young soldier visited her as she was sleeping as a dead and this soldier sat on his knees and began to cry.<br /> As a result of that this story looks last long but it is really meaningful for me. It is a portrait of forbidden love between an old lady and a young soldier and the platonic love of a doctor who loves the old lady in inside.</span>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3347504412&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3347504412" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"

<span id="freeTextContainer14164556406826722167">After “Chess”, “Amok” is the second book by Zweig I got my hands on. Both his novels started on a cruise and both times Zweig managed to make me feel on board. This time he took me on a cruise shipping from (todays) Indonesia on its way to Europe back in March 1912. Similar to “Chess” the first-person narrator ends up meeting a stranger, who is willing to tell him his secrets. Unlike in “Chess” this novel rather focuses on the unfolding story of the stranger and has only a weak – but relevant – </span>
  <span id="freeText14164556406826722167" style="display:none">After “Chess”, “Amok” is the second book by Zweig I got my hands on. Both his novels started on a cruise and both times Zweig managed to make me feel on board. This time he took me on a cruise shipping from (todays) Indonesia on its way to Europe back in March 1912. Similar to “Chess” the first-person narrator ends up meeting a stranger, who is willing to tell him his secrets. Unlike in “Chess” this novel rather focuses on the unfolding story of the stranger and has only a weak – but relevant – relationship to what is taking place on the cruise.<br /><br />Yet again I am stupefied by Zweig’s use of language; being precisely able to describe the incidents while using terms in their simplest nature. I could not help but disremember all my current obligations and deadlines once started reading “Amok”.<br /><br />A story about a man who served as a doctor in the Dutch East Indies (Indonesia) for seven years, with the objective to finish his ten year contract with the Dutch government. After which he ought to go back to Europe to enjoy the rest of his life with a generous pension. Well, this objective he could only maintain up until he met an Englishwoman; the first white woman he saw after years. When the Englishwoman begged the doctor for an abortion, the whole story of disgrace had begun. Mismanaging the request of the woman, the doctor now saw himself obligated at least to make sure the (deadly) abortion would remain as a secret for good… For the sake of keeping it as a secret, the doctor himself eventually turned to the person running “amok” on the cruise heading Europe…</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2155345206" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2155345206" />
    <a title="Brianna Wilkie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67835834-brianna-wilkie"><img alt="Brianna Wilkie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494951943p2/67835834.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2155345206?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brianna Wilkie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brianna Wilkie" href="/user/show/67835834-brianna-wilkie">Brianna Wilkie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/35163158-amok">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2155345206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11519496804810118879">Zweig's "Amok" was perhaps one of the most insightful stories I have read in a while. Even with less than a hundred pages, I was captivated in both the narrative told from the speaker as well as the mysterious acquaintance. The setting change from the night-life on the ship to the acquaitance's study were both read with a looming atmosphere that made me feel strange, to say the least. Zweig knows how to create an uncomfortable aura. I was constantly guessing what was going to happen to Frau, and</span>
  <span id="freeText11519496804810118879" style="display:none">Zweig's "Amok" was perhaps one of the most insightful stories I have read in a while. Even with less than a hundred pages, I was captivated in both the narrative told from the speaker as well as the mysterious acquaintance. The setting change from the night-life on the ship to the acquaitance's study were both read with a looming atmosphere that made me feel strange, to say the least. Zweig knows how to create an uncomfortable aura. I was constantly guessing what was going to happen to Frau, and when the acquaintance stormed into the room towards the end, I was just as involved. There were moments when the writing felt tedious - for example, when the acquaintance was discussing the ballroom and the surroundings. when we were already so invested in him trying to get back to Frau. There were other little moments such as that where the pacing felt ragged, but when the plot started to turn, it did so masterfully. This was an excellent example of a story that was to be told in the tight, compact sense, and it did so with great result. I personally will be rereading this story, and recommending it to others who enjoy a good character study.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11519496804810118879" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2762714509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9087128437419308163">The book represents a narrative which involves implementation of the concept of amok running into the life of a doctor. Amok running is a kind of pathological upheaval that leads to murders and eventually death of the suffering person. Even though there are not many scientific studies about the existence of such a trauma in modern societies, I enjoyed the narration and honestly found the story very tempting to read at once.<br/><br/>Protoganists of some stories of the writer (Chess story, Zwang, Amok etc)</span>
  <span id="freeText9087128437419308163" style="display:none">The book represents a narrative which involves implementation of the concept of amok running into the life of a doctor. Amok running is a kind of pathological upheaval that leads to murders and eventually death of the suffering person. Even though there are not many scientific studies about the existence of such a trauma in modern societies, I enjoyed the narration and honestly found the story very tempting to read at once.<br /><br />Protoganists of some stories of the writer (Chess story, Zwang, Amok etc) similarly represent pathological disorders with a natable impatience. The emotional ups and downs of those characters are always a determining factor that leads them to irrational behaviours sometimes suicide. Given the fact that Zweig himself ended up commiting suicide, the characters often look very real. <br /><br />All in all, a griping story with a sad end. If you like his style, this is a must-read story.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2759560097403223812">“'Running amok?' Yes, I think I do ...a kind of intoxication affecting the Malays...”<br/><br/>At at mere 66 pages (according to Kindle) this story really packs a punch. I thought the character development was quite good given the length of the story. I enjoyed the beginning of the book with all the descriptions of the area and the ship. I also liked the mystery of the stranger in the dark. The story then continues from the doctor's point of view and how he ended up on this ship alone heading for Europe. </span>
  <span id="freeText2759560097403223812" style="display:none">“'Running amok?' Yes, I think I do ...a kind of intoxication affecting the Malays...”<br /><br />At at mere 66 pages (according to Kindle) this story really packs a punch. I thought the character development was quite good given the length of the story. I enjoyed the beginning of the book with all the descriptions of the area and the ship. I also liked the mystery of the stranger in the dark. The story then continues from the doctor's point of view and how he ended up on this ship alone heading for Europe. I really felt the sense panic and dread from both the doctor's point of view and the woman's. The ending was a little flat and probably the weakest point of the book for me. Overall, I enjoyed the author's writing and look forward to reading more of his work. <br /><br />“...only an hour after that woman had entered my room, I had thrown my life away and was running amok, careering into empty space.”</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3103805429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16007206100247261078">Short Story? Novella? Call it what you want. I rate this one somewhere between 4 and 4.5 stars. <br/><br/>Amok is a tale of a tale. The narrator is on an ocean liner headed from India to Europe when he meets another passenger on a dark deck. This mysterious passenger hides mostly in the shadows and offers the narrator a drink and begins to tell his story. The man is a German doctor who was assigned to a remote station in the Indian countryside...hours from the nearest town. He is not living his best life.</span>
  <span id="freeText16007206100247261078" style="display:none">Short Story? Novella? Call it what you want. I rate this one somewhere between 4 and 4.5 stars. <br /><br />Amok is a tale of a tale. The narrator is on an ocean liner headed from India to Europe when he meets another passenger on a dark deck. This mysterious passenger hides mostly in the shadows and offers the narrator a drink and begins to tell his story. The man is a German doctor who was assigned to a remote station in the Indian countryside...hours from the nearest town. He is not living his best life. Then a white woman shows up at his door. The doctor wavers back and forth between his professional duty as a doctor, his personal desires and pride. So begins the doctor's slide into running amok. <br /><br />This was a very intense story that just hooked me right in. I definitely had a few "oh no he didn't!" moments. :) I will definitely look into reading more stuff by Zweig. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3P1XNcHfBEvbXIYReNLmo57dl/MaaUHIvorE7Vo80Nokhu7nCL33ZSKOAmvukR3TcIj/A6SMAsWzE5ehBKXT+g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Ünü İsviçre sınırlarını aşıp Fransa ve Almanya’ya kadar uzanmıştır. Saatçiliğin ilerleyen bilime ayak uydurmasıyla, Zacharius Usta da “saat maşası”nı icat eder. Önce tiyatro yapıtları ve opera librettoları yazdı. 1863’te Le Magasin d’Education et de Récréation’da, Voyages extraordinaire (Olağanüstü Yolculuklar) adlı dizinin ilk yapıtı olarak yayımladığı Cinq semaines en ballon (1863; Balonla Beş Hafta) büyük ilgi gördü. Bunun üzerine fantastik serüvenler yazmaya devam etti. Voyage au centre de la Terre (1864; Dünyanın Merkezine Yolculuk), Vingt mille lieues sous les mers (1870; Deniz Altında Yirmi Bin Fersah) ve L’Île mystérieuse (1874; Esrarlı Ada) bunlardan bazılarıdır. Pek çok yapıtı arasında en çok ilgi çeken romanı Le Tour du monde en quatre-vingts jours (1873; Seksen Günde Dünya Gezisi ) bugüne dek popülaritesini korumuştur.\n\nRomanları çok sayıda dile çevrilen <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13683437967325183149">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br/><br/>Zweig studied in Austria, France</span>
  <span id="freeText13683437967325183149" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br /><br />Zweig studied in Austria, France, and Germany before settling in Salzburg in 1913. In 1934, driven into exile by the Nazis, he emigrated to England and then, in 1940, to Brazil by way of New York. Finding only growing loneliness and disillusionment in their new surroundings, he and his second wife committed suicide.<br /><br />Zweig's interest in psychology and the teachings of Sigmund Freud led to his most characteristic work, the subtle portrayal of character. Zweig's essays include studies of Honoré de Balzac, Charles Dickens, and Fyodor Dostoyevsky (<i>Drei Meister</i>, 1920; <i>Three Masters</i>) and of Friedrich Hölderlin, Heinrich von Kleist, and Friedrich Nietzsche (<i>Der Kampf mit dem Dämon</i>, 1925; <i>Master Builders</i>). He achieved popularity with <i>Sternstunden der Menschheit</i> (1928; <i>The Tide of Fortune</i>), five historical portraits in miniature. He wrote full-scale, intuitive rather than objective, biographies of the French statesman Joseph Fouché (1929), Mary Stuart (1935), and others. His stories include those in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> (1925; <i>Conflicts</i>). He also wrote a psychological novel, <i>Ungeduld des Herzens</i> (1938; <i>Beware of Pity</i>), and translated works of Charles Baudelaire, Paul Verlaine, and Emile Verhaeren.<br /><br />Most recently, his works provided the inspiration for 2014 film <i>The Grand Budapest Hotel</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13683437967325183149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
